The solid (dashed) lines are for a uniform circular source with radius 0.1\u00a0kpc (1\u00a0kpc).\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig11.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096317/stv2791fig11.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig11.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096317\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig11.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=ldbR8APNb5kS6rIs0NxjO6OZkhYM5BXJm2HAJMZd11zcL1WBspVc65zv2Fj4t8Wxib3-N1cRMleKQZKq10xXQcRKqkODXBFvIhM9NwlFgtMg5tD4wLsu7XPbbzmbpHm-2e09URu3SkewaBRphss6xrOmqE1k0arWXwr4mEbSL1tfk5xTzVM4gdKiFOGQerQPSvqE2h1fLyJtnum0lQq7mrf4e2gxJ3TK3HE21KSuQ6q~zFc1gTocogxl0D2WNNA4v8tlu2~lG6SqNoT9iiKO63NrkSrnzXo~a~s4koBsw8ub1pbX5S-O8ozgSs739HMGsEE4eP6OceRr2X0aHZgSUg__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096317&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig11.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The optical depth for magnification causing changes in the apparent magnitude (\u0394m) of an extended source by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. The solid (dashed) lines are for a uniform circular source with radius 0.1\u00a0kpc (1\u00a0kpc).</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096318 id=\"92096318\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>CONCLUSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we have investigated compound lensing by two lens galaxies and a further source, at different redshifts, and the image morphologies this can generate. We found that compound lensing can produce high-multiplicity and high-magnification events. Discovering a high-multiplicity lensed quasar would be an extremely powerful cosmological probe, since many independent time-delays can be measured. High-magnification events have the power to uncover the population of ultrafaint high-redshift sources that are otherwise far beyond the depth and resolution of current telescopes.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We found analytic results for the case of two SIS lenses, solving completely for the case when the observer, source and both lenses lie on a plane. From this, we found that the highest multiplicity images can only form if both (a) the second lens is multiply imaged by the first lens and (b) the Einstein radius of the second lens is comparable to, but less than the Einstein radius of the first lens. We showed numerically that when the source lies off of the plane, some lens configurations can generate higher order catastrophes that produce extreme magnifications \u2013 a potentially important method for discovering and studying intrinsically faint high-redshift sources. The simple picture painted by the two SIS model is complicated significantly when the lenses are elliptically flattened. Up to 16 image configurations can exist if the second lens is quadruply imaged by the first lens.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">With this analytic understanding of the system, we then used the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> code of Collett (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) to answers our three primary questions. </p><ul class=\"bullet\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">How often does compound lensing generate high-multiplicity systems? We found that the optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens is \u2243 6\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22126</sup> for 3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a010. Image multiplicities of six or greater are\u00a0\u223c2.2 orders of magnitude rarer. A wide-deep survey like LSST which should detect\u00a0\u223c10<sup>10</sup> galaxies beyond redshift 2 (Ivezic et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>), and is therefore likely to include some high-multiplicity lenses. This may be an underestimate, since arguments based on optical depths do not include magnification bias.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">How often does compound lensing generate extreme magnifications? We found \u22432\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22126</sup> of the 3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a010 sky has its flux increased by more than 2 mag. Magnification of 100 (decreasing the apparent magnitude by five) is 2.5 orders of magnitude rarer for 0.1\u00a0kpc radius sources, but is almost impossible for sources with 1\u00a0kpc radii. Under simplifying assumptions, we inferred that <em>Euclid</em> may discover one <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a08 source that has been magnified by a hundred.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">What do high-multiplicity/magnification compound lenses look like?</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For low-multiplicity/magnification compound lenses, the image configurations are typically perturbed versions of single-plane image morphologies. However, for high-multiplicity/magnification compound lenses, the image morphologies are significantly more complicated; even with two SIE profiles the parameter space is rich, and many images can form. We show 20 high-multiplicity configurations in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig12\" data-open=\"fig12\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">12</a>.</p></li></ul>                        <a id=\"92096323\" scrollto-destination=\"92096323\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig12\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 12.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig12.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=2RxCsGAqVN-Ler~zt426Sn-xHdZiVHNEHiWDdI9P5Zx0-Vwp7~TU~qdpPTYPjcYGY0JO92mB-r2ay5-Gk1S-B27f8baW~rC64nowYh63Y2-pDpf~EmoLsCFgbT88OSL7t4Cv2LRGNKbtpWRpFPWF~4tBcyXolFJmYnJe102H1tBheX5oZuRA4yBwhrS-Zjuo6TtihcaF5Ey1Ll9C0FbpTUrHPTat2ASlsZBRtxws1lmma2KOhU68tc356-WKCQt6mBUgAXl69jGOCtz6v7MpQ7eQA~20RXwOEDvsZU-ML~pEWWgTNKrZoUleVF5e-NoiQAwdNRVTDj6W6vG6jOKuyw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"A montage of high-multiplicity compound lenses generated from the lenspop population of plausible deflectors. The optical centre of the first lens is shown as a red cross.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096323/stv2791fig12.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096323\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig12.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=lp38igRDBDmZGr8UbCNwElXfSxEySbDtZWTrKpsLbots0pU4Jho8TIC9RZDhW~YIq-K7O0s1LYuzQZ5xCtWyz~V-sQtj~aELFaHNnghbX5FtHw~GYy-hTCY6BI5FnfhyDfG8Ty~CAtwLGRtAoUyDPshDVQ22AOCcIbObuok4VEwerYfy7OeP2pPgDTeXmG~9CqT0o2dqSvr1HqUEZ5OCSe-MHE5NhEn-hp2mk5DvK8YtFNW8zmT~seoBxVCOGw7JhQfphaMcOJtz6bDWqBqW31H1zh3Mq8q~6-G9y9UIqP3Lf31HI1Il2gWnwzcqoMzcvirYwg5Aha3tpCG6Ji7eiA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096323&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">A montage of high-multiplicity compound lenses generated from the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> population of plausible deflectors. The optical centre of the first lens is shown as a red cross.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096323/stv2791fig12.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096323\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig12.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=lp38igRDBDmZGr8UbCNwElXfSxEySbDtZWTrKpsLbots0pU4Jho8TIC9RZDhW~YIq-K7O0s1LYuzQZ5xCtWyz~V-sQtj~aELFaHNnghbX5FtHw~GYy-hTCY6BI5FnfhyDfG8Ty~CAtwLGRtAoUyDPshDVQ22AOCcIbObuok4VEwerYfy7OeP2pPgDTeXmG~9CqT0o2dqSvr1HqUEZ5OCSe-MHE5NhEn-hp2mk5DvK8YtFNW8zmT~seoBxVCOGw7JhQfphaMcOJtz6bDWqBqW31H1zh3Mq8q~6-G9y9UIqP3Lf31HI1Il2gWnwzcqoMzcvirYwg5Aha3tpCG6Ji7eiA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096323&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">A montage of high-multiplicity compound lenses generated from the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> population of plausible deflectors. The black crosses show the location that images of a point source would form, while the central region of an extended source is shown in light blue, with the outer regions shown in red. If the second lens is associated with an extended light profile (assumed to be circular), it would appear as the dark blue region. The optical centre of the first lens is shown as a red cross.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this work, we have assumed a population of isothermal ellipsoids in an otherwise homogeneous Universe. Deviations from isothermality and elliptical symmetry and the perturbative effects of mass along the line of sight will further increase the complexity of compound lenses that are possible in the real Universe. Such complexity is beyond the scope of this work, but we note that in principle this can further increase magnifications and image multiplicity. Where lenses are less steeply cusped the pseudo-caustics in Figs\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a> and\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a> become classical caustics, and it is possible for detectable central images to form near the core of the primary lens or the images of the secondary. For aligned spherical lenses, an Einstein ring forms at \u03b8<sub><em>E</em>1</sub>\u00a0+\u00a0\u03b8<sub><em>E</em>2</sub> if both lenses are isothermal; the radius is larger if both lenses are subisothermal. At small impact parameters, the deflection angles are increased for steeper-than-isothermal lenses. We therefore expect that shallower profiles are more efficient at producing low-multiplicity compound lenses, while steeper profiles produce more Einstein zig-zags.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">There is a real danger that current lens finding algorithms would fail to detect any of the high-multiplicity compound lenses shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig12\" data-open=\"fig12\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">12</a>. Strong lens finders based on forward modelling such as that in Brault &amp; Gavazzi (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) would not be able to fit most of the compound lenses with a single-plane lens model. Compound lenses may be more easily found by finders that look for ring- or arc-like features such as Gavazzi et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) or Seidel &amp; Bartelmann (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>), since higher multiplicity lenses imply more chances for the features to be present. The main problem however is sample purity; the rarity of strong lensing means that most lens finders will severely cut the pool of candidates to remove the large numbers of false positives. Algorithms that remove false positives from lens samples are likely to also remove some of the compound lenses. The most exotic lenses will require novel discovery algorithms, or an extensive and imaginative human search.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We are grateful to Charlotte Mason for calculating the size of the compound lens population discoverable by <em>Euclid</em>. We thank the anonymous referee for an extremely constructive report on the original manuscript. We are grateful to Charlotte Mason and Bob Nichol for useful discussions and comments on the draft manuscript. We are grateful to Matt Auger for the lensing deflections code. We are grateful to Thomas Tram for improvements to our caustic tracing code. This work has used the LSST sky simulations; we are grateful to Andy Connolly for sharing these, and to Scott Daniel and Gary Burton for enabling access to them. This project made use of the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> code which is freely available from github.com/tcollett/LensPop. Numerical computations were done on the Sciama High Performance Compute (HPC) cluster which is supported by the ICG, SEPNet and the University of Portsmouth.</p><div content-id=\"fn1\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn1\" class=\"end-note-link\">1</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">Rearranging equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>) into a single equation gives an eighth-order polynomial.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn2\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn2\" class=\"end-note-link\">2</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">A pseudo-caustic is a caustic, but crossing it only creates one detectable additional image, since the second additional image is infinitely demagnified at the core of a singularity.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn3\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn3\" class=\"end-note-link\">3</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">That is to say, there are 1.3\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>7</sup> strong lenses in the Universe with second components sufficiently bright and massive to be included in the catalogue of Connolly et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) \u2013 of course most are undetectable with current instrumentation.</p></div></span></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096332 id=\"92096332\" class=\"backreferences-title\" >REFERENCES</h2>\r\n<div class=\"ref-list\"><div content-id=\"bib1\" data-legacy-id=\"bib1\"><div class=\"refLink-parent\"><span class=\"refLink\"><a name=\"jumplink-bib1\" href=\"javascript:;\" aria-label=\"jumplink-bib1\"></a></span></div><div class=\"ref false\"><div id=\"ref-auto-bib1\" class=\"ref-content \"><div class=\"mixed-citation citation\"><p class=\"mixed-citation-compatibility\">\r\n<span class=\"person-group\">\r\n<div class=\"name\">\r\n<div class=\"surname\">Auger</div>\r\n <div class=\"given-names\">M. type=\"hidden\" value=\"mnras\" /> \n    </div>\n\r\n                    </div>\r\n                </div>\r\n            </div>\r\n            <div id=\"ContentTab\" class=\"content active\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096271 id=\"92096271\" class=\"abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Compound strong gravitational lensing is a rare phenomenon, but a handful of such lensed systems are likely to be discovered in forthcoming surveys. In this work, we use a double singular isothermal sphere lens model to analytically understand how the properties of the system impact image multiplicity for the final source. We find that up to six images of a background source can form, but only if the second lens is multiply imaged by the first and the Einstein radius of the second lens is comparable to, but does not exceed that of the first. We then build a model of compound lensing masses in the Universe, using singular isothermal ellipsoid (SIE) lenses, and assess how the optical depth for multiple imaging by a galaxy\u2013galaxy compound lens varies with source redshift. For a source redshift of 4, we find optical depths of 6\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22126</sup> for multiple imaging and 5\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22128</sup> for multiplicity of 6 or greater. We find that extreme magnifications are possible, with magnifications of 100 or more for 6\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22129</sup> of <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a010 sources with 0.1\u00a0kpc radii. We show some of the image configurations that can be generated by compound lenses, and demonstrate that they are qualitatively different to those generated by single-plane lenses; dedicated compound lens finders will be necessary if these systems are to be discovered in forthcoming surveys.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096273 id=\"92096273\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Strong gravitational lensing is a rare phenomenon, but forthcoming surveys are forecast to discover hundreds of thousands of strong lenses in the next decade (Collett <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). This huge strong lens population will include a subset of \u2013 extremely rare \u2013 exotic strong lenses, where higher order lensing catastrophes create six or more images of a single background source. Exotic lenses are primarily interesting for three reasons: they are often powerful cosmic telescopes (e.g. Orban de Xivry &amp; Marshall <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Wong et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) with extreme source magnifications near the catastrophes; the image configuration is extremely sensitive to the dark matter distribution in the lens(es) (e.g. Orban de Xivry &amp; Marshall <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>), and if the lenses are at different redshifts, or the sources are time-variable they are powerful cosmological probes (e.g. Collett et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Collett &amp; Auger <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The simple picture of lensing by an elliptically symmetrical mass distribution with monotonically decreasing density gives rise to at most five images of a background point source (Evans &amp; Witt <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>). However, when multiple galaxies play a significant role in the lensing, the formation of additional images (e.g. Keeton, Mao &amp; Witt <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>) is possible. Recently several exotic lenses have been discovered; at cluster scales systems with more than five images are not uncommon. For example, SDSS J2222+2745 is a cluster lensing a quasar into six images (Dahle et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), the cluster-lensed supernova <em>\u2018Refsdal\u2019</em> is predicted to be sextuply imaged (Kelly et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), and Abell 1703 contains an ultrarare hyperbolic umbilic catastrophe (Orban de Xivry &amp; Marshall <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>). For less massive systems, sextuples are harder to create but can occur for binary lenses (e.g. Shin &amp; Evans <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) or for individual galaxies with multipolar structures (Evans &amp; Witt <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>), such as galaxies with significant bulge and disc components (Orban de Xivry &amp; Marshall <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Theoretical work on understanding lensing exotica has focused on systems with a single lens plane; however, it is also possible for exotica to occur in compound lenses. Kochanek &amp; Apostolakis (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1988</a>) first considered lensing by two isothermal spheres on different planes; finding seven image configurations for certain lens and source parameters, but their investigation was limited by the computational power then available. Werner, An &amp; Evans (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) considered the same model and showed that it is possible to create multiple Einstein rings of a background source, if all three objects lie on a single optical axis, and the Einstein radius of the two lenses are comparable. M\u00f6ller &amp; Blain (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) also considered compound lensing by galaxies, finding that roughly 1 in 20 multiply imaged sources will be a compound lens with merging caustics.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">A handful of galaxy\u2013galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses are now known (Belokurov et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>; Gavazzi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>). Early work on the double source plane lens SDSSJ0946+1006 by Gavazzi et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) and Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) preferred non-zero lensing effect by the first source acting on the second. Recently a full reconstruction of the lensed arcs by Collett &amp; Auger (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) measured the Einstein radius of the first source to be 0.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02 arcsec, even without fixing the cosmological parameters; this demonstrates that there is indeed a compound lensing effect in this system.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The masses and positions in SDSSJ0946+1006 give rise to nothing more exotic than an Einstein ring from each source, but it is likely that the hundreds of thousands of strong lenses discoverable in future surveys will contain exotic compound lenses. In this work, we aim to understand the configurations, magnifications and abundances of exotic compound lenses that are likely to be discovered in the near future.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this work, we are primarily concerned with answering three questions. </p><ul class=\"bullet\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">How often does compound lensing generate high-multiplicity systems?</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">How often does compound lensing generate extreme magnifications?</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">What do high-multiplicity/magnification compound lenses look like?</p></li></ul><p class=\"chapter-para\">The paper is organized as follows. In Section\u00a02, we review the general theory of compound lensing, which we apply to the case of two singular isothermal sphere lenses in Section\u00a03. We provide analytical results for the case of the observer, lenses and source lying on a plane in section\u00a03.1, and show numerical results for the general system in Section\u00a03.2. In Section\u00a04, we investigate the effect of lens ellipticities on the system, and calculate optical depths for high-multiplicity and high-magnification compound lensing. We discuss our results, and answer the primary questions above in Section\u00a05.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096282 id=\"92096282\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>COMPOUND LENSING</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this section, we briefly review the theory of compound lensing, following Schneider, Ehlers &amp; Falco (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1992</a>).</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">For a single-source-plane lens, the lens equation can be written as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ1\" content-id=\"equ1\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}}_2 = D_2/D_1 \\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}}_1 - D_{12}\\hat{\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} }_1(\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}}_1),\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(1)</span></div>where <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}_1}$|</span> is the physical position vector in the image plane, <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}_2}$|</span> is the unlensed source position vector, and <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|${{\\hat{\\alpha }_1}(\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}_1})}$|</span> is the physical deflection a ray undergoes as it passes through <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}_1}$|\u2060</span>. <em>D</em><sub>\u03bc\u03bd</sub> are angular diameter distances between planes \u03bc and \u03bd. Once a light ray has been traced back to the second plane, we can trace it back to the third, remembering that the second deflection is a function of where it passes through the second plane. Recursively repeating this allows us to write down the multiple-plane lens equation <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ2\" content-id=\"equ2\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}}_\\nu = D_\\nu /D_1 \\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}}_1 - \\sum _{\\mu =1}^{\\nu -1}D_{\\mu \\nu }\\hat{\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} }_\\mu (\\boldsymbol {\\hat{x}}_\\mu ).\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(2)</span></div>The multiple-plane lens equation is more elegantly written in angular quantities by rescaling the physical deflections to their angular effect on a ray from the final source plane, <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ3\" content-id=\"equ3\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} }_\\mu = \\frac{D_{\\mu s}}{D_{s}}\\hat{{\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} }}_\\mu .\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(3)</span></div>This gives <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ4\" content-id=\"equ4\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {x}_\\nu =\\boldsymbol {x}_1 - \\sum _{\\mu =1}^{\\nu -1}\\beta _{\\mu \\nu }{\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} }_{\\mu }(\\boldsymbol {x}_\\mu ),\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(4)</span></div>where <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {x}_\\nu$|</span> is the angular position on plane \u03bd. \u03b2<sub>\u03bc\u03bd</sub> is defined as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ5\" content-id=\"equ5\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\beta _{\\mu \\nu } \\equiv \\frac{D_{\\mu \\nu } D_{s}}{D_{\\nu } D_{\\mu s}}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(5)</span></div>Since, in this paper, we are only interested in the case of a three-plane system (see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>), we will use the shorthand <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ6\" content-id=\"equ6\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\beta \\equiv \\frac{D_{12} D_{3}}{D_{2} D_{23}}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(6)</span></div>In the case of a three-plane system, the lens equation for photons originating on the second plane is <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ7\" content-id=\"equ7\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {y} = \\boldsymbol {x} - \\beta {\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} _{\\rm {1}}}(\\boldsymbol {x}),\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(7)</span></div>where <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {y}$|</span> is the unlensed position of the source on plane 2. For photons originating on the third plane, <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ8\" content-id=\"equ8\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {z} = \\boldsymbol {x} - {\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} _{\\rm {1}}}(\\boldsymbol {x}) - {\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} _{\\rm {2}}}(\\boldsymbol {x}- \\beta {\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} _{\\rm {1}}}(\\boldsymbol {x})),\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(8)</span></div>where <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {z}$|</span> is the unlensed position of the source on plane 3.</div>                        <a id=\"92096285\" scrollto-destination=\"92096285\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=I~jYrQmNYXAS5d~EW6WhxForFEQe7JIULG7QQyjf3TthHNcF~7M-jwfF5hYyFepS3ql7nuEZ98ZWgDT3lc5uMpmjslRnYORukjdX~3kjFn76wuKIvzfKp6R7UDgJsn1fcPFY4jTF0GHoRszXGyTLr5EErBbSM~7Aavf0UK-3EE2RS5eNMkems1pf9TXTXyT3Laim4kELNiOUD6URmUOdDaeSSWqpdWoWH8cPqUT9v9eFLadxF93BEwsyUeyPrAFCw7573qkmMXgf7i-EMBOpxT7OZs2qZvdr9rpY7nLzS5W~4DOG~FEQpEWUUwpWj8n5ZPF-A4u8N8MI9ekXLuERQQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The configuration considered for a two component lens. The observer O is set at the far left of the figure. The primary lens lies on the optical axis, with angular coordinates (0, 0); the location of the second lens defines the second axis and lies at (<em>y</em>, 0). The source lies at <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$(z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{{\\imath }}}}, z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{{\\jmath }}}})$|</span> with the <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}}$|</span> coordinate in the direction perpendicular to the plane of the page. Quantities shown on the diagram are physical (hence hatted).</p></div></div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Throughout this work, we will refer to angular positions on the image plane, <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {x_1}$|\u2060</span>, as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ9\" content-id=\"equ9\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {x_1}={\\left(\\begin{array}{cc}x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{{\\imath }}}} \\\\\r\n x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{{\\jmath }}}} \\end{array}\\right)}={\\left(\\begin{array}{cc}r \\cos (\\theta ) \\\\\r\n r \\sin (\\theta ) \\end{array}\\right)}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(9)</span></div>We will work in units scaled to \u03b1<sub>1</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01 unless otherwise specified.</div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096287 id=\"92096287\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>COMPOUND LENSING BY TWO ISOTHERMAL SPHERES</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">For simplicity, we focus primarily on the case where both lenses are singular isothermal spheres (SISs). For this model, we can analytically derive some key results. A schematic of our system is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. Elliptical galaxies dominate the strong lensing cross-section, and have been shown to be approximately isothermal in their total density profile (Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>), so the analytic results for the double SIS model can give us intuition for more general compound lensing.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The deflection angles of an SIS have the analytically convenient property that their magnitude is constant, and the direction always points at the lensing centre: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ10\" content-id=\"equ10\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\boldsymbol {\\alpha} }(\\boldsymbol {r})=\\alpha \\hat{\\boldsymbol {r}}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(10)</span></div>Symmetry allows us to place the first lens on the optical axis, and the second lens at <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ11\" content-id=\"equ11\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {y}={\\left(\\begin{array}{c}y \\\\\r\n 0 \\end{array}\\right)},\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(11)</span></div>insisting that <em>y</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00. The light associated with lens 2 is thus observed at <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {x_1}=(x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{\\mathbf {\\imath }}}}, 0)$|</span><div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ12\" content-id=\"equ12\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\ny= x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} - \\beta \\alpha _1 x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}/|x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}|.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(12)</span></div>For <em>x</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00, this gives a solution with an image at <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ13\" content-id=\"equ13\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nx_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} = y+ \\beta \\alpha _1,\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(13)</span></div>which always exists since <em>y</em>, \u03b2 and \u03b1<sub>1</sub> are all positive. A second solution exists for <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}&lt;0$|</span> at <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ14\" content-id=\"equ14\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nx_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} = y - \\beta \\alpha _1,\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(14)</span></div>if and only if <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ15\" content-id=\"equ15\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\ny&lt; \\beta \\alpha _1.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(15)</span></div>Equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ15\"></span><a href=\"#equ15\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">15</a>) is thus the condition for multiple imaging of the lens residing on plane 2.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The final source is at <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {z}=(z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}, z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}})$|\u2060</span>. By symmetry we can insist that <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}}&gt;0$|\u2060</span>, but <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}$|</span> can take any value. The deflection caused by the second SIS is given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ16\" content-id=\"equ16\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\boldsymbol \\alpha }_2(\\boldsymbol {x}_2)=\\alpha _2 \\widehat{\\left(\\boldsymbol {x}_2-\\boldsymbol {y}\\right)}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(16)</span></div>Since <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {x}_2=\\boldsymbol {x}_1 - \\beta \\alpha _1 \\hat{\\boldsymbol {x}}_1$|\u2060</span>, substituting into equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ8\"></span><a href=\"#equ8\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">8</a>) quickly becomes unwieldy. It is much more convenient to write <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\boldsymbol {x}_1$|</span> in polar coordinates, centred on the first lens, <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ17\" content-id=\"equ17\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {x}_1={\\left(\\begin{array}{c}r \\cos {\\theta }\\\\\r\n r \\sin {\\theta } \\end{array}\\right)},\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(17)</span></div>giving <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ18\" content-id=\"equ18\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\boldsymbol {x}_2={\\left(\\begin{array}{c}\\cos {\\theta }\\\\\r\n\\sin {\\theta } \\end{array}\\right)}(r-\\beta \\alpha _1)\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(18)</span></div>and <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ19\" content-id=\"equ19\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\widehat{\\boldsymbol {x}_2-\\boldsymbol {y}}= \\frac{r-\\beta \\alpha _1}{D} {\\left(\\begin{array}{c}\\cos {\\theta }\\\\\r\n\\sin {\\theta } \\end{array}\\right)}-\\frac{1}{D}{\\left(\\begin{array}{c}y\\\\\r\n0 \\end{array}\\right)}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(19)</span></div>with <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ20\" content-id=\"equ20\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nD=\\left((r-\\beta \\alpha _1)^2+y^2-2y(r-\\beta \\alpha _1)\\cos {\\theta } \\right)^{1/2}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(20)</span></div>Substituting into equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ8\"></span><a href=\"#equ8\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">8</a>) gives <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ21\" content-id=\"equ21\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\left(\\begin{array}{ccc}z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} \\\\\r\nz_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}} \\end{array}\\right)}=\\left(r-\\alpha _1-\\frac{\\alpha _2(r-\\beta \\alpha _1)}{D}\\right){\\left(\\begin{array}{ccc}\\cos {\\theta }\\\\\r\n\\sin {\\theta } \\end{array}\\right)}-\\frac{\\alpha _2}{D}{\\left(\\begin{array}{ccc}y\\\\\r\n0 \\end{array}\\right)}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(21)</span></div>Equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>) now allows us to solve for the location and multiplicity of images for any source position.</div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=92096291 id=\"92096291\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1>Analytic solutions for the case <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}}=0$|</span></h3>\r\n<div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Simple solutions for (<em>r</em>, \u03b8) can be obtained for the case where the lenses and source lie on a plane i.e. <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}}=0$|\u2060</span>. The <em>j</em> component of equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>) is thus <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ22\" content-id=\"equ22\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n0=\\left(r-\\alpha _1-\\frac{\\alpha _2(r-\\beta \\alpha _1)}{D}\\right)\\sin {\\theta }.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(22)</span></div>Trivially, there are a set of solutions to equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>) with sin\u2009\u03b8\u00a0=\u00a00, and hence cos\u2009\u03b8\u00a0=\u00a0\u00b11 and <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}}=0$|\u2060</span>. We can thus simplify equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>) to <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ23\" content-id=\"equ23\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nz_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=\\left(x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} \\mp \\alpha _1\\right)-\\frac{\\alpha _2}{D}\\frac{x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} -y \\mp \\beta \\alpha _1}{|x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} -y \\mp \\beta \\alpha _1|}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(23)</span></div>which has up to four real solutions at <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ24\" content-id=\"equ24\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nx_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}\\pm \\alpha _1 \\pm \\alpha _2;\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(24)</span></div>however, the four solutions do not always exist. In turn the conditions are <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ25\" content-id=\"equ25\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\nx_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} + \\alpha _1 + \\alpha _2 &amp;\\quad \\mathrm{exists} \\: \\mathrm{if} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &gt; y-(\\alpha _2+(1-\\beta )\\alpha _1) \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp; \\mathrm{and} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &gt; -(\\alpha _1+\\alpha _2)\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(25)</span></div><div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ26\" content-id=\"equ26\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\nx_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} - \\alpha _1 - \\alpha _2 &amp;\\quad \\mathrm{exists} \\: \\mathrm{if} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &lt; y+(\\alpha _2+(1-\\beta )\\alpha _1) \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp; \\mathrm{and} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &lt; +(\\alpha _1+\\alpha _2)\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(26)</span></div><div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ27\" content-id=\"equ27\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\nx_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} - \\alpha _1 + \\alpha _2 &amp;\\quad \\mathrm{exists} \\: \\mathrm{if} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &gt; y-(\\alpha _2-(1-\\beta )\\alpha _1) \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp; \\mathrm{and} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &lt; (\\alpha _1-\\alpha _2)\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(27)</span></div><div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ28\" content-id=\"equ28\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\nx_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} + \\alpha _1 - \\alpha _2 &amp;\\quad \\mathrm{exists} \\: \\mathrm{if} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &lt; y+(\\alpha _2-(1-\\beta )\\alpha _1) \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp; \\mathrm{and} &amp;\\quad z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} &gt; -(\\alpha _1-\\alpha _2).\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(28)</span></div>For the case of <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=0$|</span> and <em>y</em>\u00a0=\u00a00, we recover the result of Werner et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>), that an inner Einstein ring forms only if <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ29\" content-id=\"equ29\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n1-\\beta &lt;\\alpha _2/\\alpha _1&lt;1.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(29)</span></div>If all four of these images form, two light rays from the source must cross the optical axis between the two lenses, since equations (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ27\"></span><a href=\"#equ27\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">27</a>) and (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ28\"></span><a href=\"#equ28\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">28</a>) require the ray to pass to the left of one lens and the right of the other; we call such systems Einstein zig-zags. Zig-zags form only if equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ29\"></span><a href=\"#equ29\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">29</a>) holds and both <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ30\" content-id=\"equ30\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n|z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}-y|&lt; \\alpha _2 - \\beta \\alpha _1\\quad {\\rm and }\\quad |z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}|&lt; \\alpha _1-\\alpha _2\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(30)</span></div>are satisfied. Rearranging equations (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ25\"></span><a href=\"#equ25\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">25</a>) through (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ28\"></span><a href=\"#equ28\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">28</a>) into constraints on <em>y</em>, we recover |<em>y</em>|\u00a0&lt;\u00a0\u03b2\u03b1<sub>1</sub>; for an Einstein zig-zag to form, it is necessary (but not sufficient) that the second lens is multiply imaged by the first. Equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ29\"></span><a href=\"#equ29\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">29</a>) is sensible; it requires each lens to be able to refocus a pair of light rays defocused by the other lens.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Equations (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ25\"></span><a href=\"#equ25\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">25</a>)\u2013(<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ28\"></span><a href=\"#equ28\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">28</a>) give the location of images forming with \u03b8\u00a0=\u00a00 or \u03c0, i.e. on the plane of the lenses and sources. Images can potentially form off of this plane if equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ22\"></span><a href=\"#equ22\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">22</a>) can be satisfied for sin\u2009\u03b8 \u2260 0. Equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ22\"></span><a href=\"#equ22\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">22</a>) then implies <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ31\" content-id=\"equ31\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nD=\\frac{\\alpha _2\\left(r-\\beta \\alpha _1\\right)}{r-\\alpha _1}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(31)</span></div>which can be substituted into the <em>i</em> component of equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>) to give <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ32\" content-id=\"equ32\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nz_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=\\frac{\\alpha _2 y}{D} = \\frac{r-\\alpha _1}{r- \\beta \\alpha _1} y;\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(32)</span></div>hence, the images must form at radius <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ33\" content-id=\"equ33\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nr=\\alpha _1\\frac{\\beta z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} -y}{z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}-y}.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(33)</span></div>Curiously, the radius on which the <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$x_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}} \\ne 0$|</span> images form is independent of \u03b1<sub>2</sub>.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Substituting <em>r</em> back into equations (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ20\"></span><a href=\"#equ20\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">20</a>) and (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ31\"></span><a href=\"#equ31\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">31</a>), we have <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ34\" content-id=\"equ34\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\n\\frac{D^2}{y^2}=\\left(\\frac{\\alpha _2}{z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}}\\right)^2=\\left(\\frac{\\alpha _1\\left(\\beta -1\\right)}{z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}-y}\\right)^2+1-2\\left(\\frac{\\alpha _1\\left(\\beta -1\\right)}{z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}-y}\\right)\\cos {\\theta } \\nonumber\\\\\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(34)</span></div>which gives <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ35\" content-id=\"equ35\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\cos {\\theta }=\\frac{1}{2\\alpha _1}\\left(\\alpha _1^2\\frac{\\beta -1}{z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}-y}+\\left(1-\\left(\\frac{\\alpha _2}{z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}}\\right)^2\\right) \\frac{z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}-y}{\\beta -1} \\right).\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(35)</span></div>Since <em>z</em>, <em>y</em>, \u03b2, \u03b1<sub>1</sub> and \u03b1<sub>2</sub>, take single values for any lens configuration, cos\u2009\u03b8 has a single value, and hence, at most two images can form additionally to those in equations (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ25\"></span><a href=\"#equ25\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">25</a>)\u2013(<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ28\"></span><a href=\"#equ28\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">28</a>). For the off-plane images to form, we must have \u22121\u00a0&lt;\u00a0cos\u2009\u03b8\u00a0&lt;\u00a01, <em>r</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00 and <em>D</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00. The <em>r</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00 constraint implies that off-plane images will not form if <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\beta z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}&lt;y&lt;z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}$|</span> and the <em>D</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00 constraint implies off-plane images will not form if <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00, but we have been unable to further simplify the inequalities for when the off-axis images do form.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Equations (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ25\"></span><a href=\"#equ25\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">25</a>)\u2013(<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ28\"></span><a href=\"#equ28\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">28</a>) and equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ35\"></span><a href=\"#equ35\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">35</a>) show that up to six images of the background source can form depending on the values of <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}},y,\\beta ,\\alpha _1$|</span> and \u03b1<sub>2</sub>. Forming 6<sup>+2</sup> detectable images only happens in a small range of the parameter space; we show an example case in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> \u2013 we will call this a 6<sup>+2</sup> image system since there are six images of the background source and two images of the second lens. In this system, we have a lens with two sources at comparable redshift (\u03b2\u00a0=\u00a00.9), the first source is slightly offset from the optical axis (<em>y</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.1) and lenses the second source (at <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\imath }}}}=0.2$|\u2060</span>) by a tenth of the Einstein radius of the first lens.</p>                        <a id=\"92096296\" scrollto-destination=\"92096296\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=YkcA2KGUKHnaGx7v~gWqb9vscR3pAK2m-GEeAWSttGI4hz9ja5BEG9Jk-OjP1h9IvdPGFsPbDC0mrJNXQnRXs6DFefkGoXx14Y6aNBcijXV5wGjZpL-UdvRp5PaO6EOpd3OFyLCSH4x-D78S3Fmybg2ZgeI7Jeld9DGNBBk0lyN~ZwW-G0bJzLbWtt8tS8x10qgon41zIcsMUtnR-ccQ3WUZ6pYIlbd~4ngvYCP~MYeJ1S4P3WFpUhU-SISada1WqJfgUFfcXVcXYpeeTS3i16Z9QpzLtAV80lfgU0BN8TCrIqEXCR6GJibYjA3P1GAO9YN4AnyLa18jyA4nArPKFg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The image plane for a 6+2 image system. The system has \u03b11\u00a0=\u00a01, \u03b12\u00a0=\u00a00.2, \u03b2\u00a0=\u00a00.9, y\u00a0=\u00a00.1 and a circular source centred at $\\boldsymbol {z}=(0.2,0)$, with radius 0.05. The black and blue circles show the unlensed position of the sources on planes 2 and 3. The black and blue circles show the unlensed position of the sources on planes 2 and 3. The red cross shows the centre of the first lens, the black crosses show where images of the second lens would form.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=92096297 id=\"92096297\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-2>Numerical solutions for the case <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$z_{\\boldsymbol{\\hat{ {\\jmath }}}} \\ne 0$|</span></h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">When the source lies off of the plane described by the observer and the two lenses, it is not possible to analytically solve for the image positions<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a>; however, the image positions can be found numerically. Shin &amp; Evans (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) showed that the caustic structure of the single-plane binary lens is complicated and sensitive to the properties of the lenses with up to seven images forming. Topologically, the compound lens and the binary lens are similar, but the binary lens cannot create the equivalent topology of an Einstein zig-zag (where more than one light ray zig-zags between the two lenses) \u2013 see Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. The analytical solutions for sources on the <em>z</em><sub><em>j</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00 line, tell us that zig-zags can only form if equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ29\"></span><a href=\"#equ29\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">29</a>) holds, and if the second lens is multiply imaged by the first. For an SIS lens, the zig-zag critical curves collapse to the very centres of the first lens and the two images of the second lens; we can approximate these by circles of very small radius. Lensing these critical curves back to the source plane gives three pseudo-caustics<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a>; if a source is inside all three of these pseudo-caustics, and equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ29\"></span><a href=\"#equ29\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">29</a>) is satisfied, an Einstein zig-zag forms (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>.1). The critical curves around the images of the second lens map into circles on the source plane with radius equal to the Einstein radius of the second lens and centred on <em>y</em>\u00a0\u00b1\u00a0(1\u00a0\u2212\u00a0\u03b2)\u03b1<sub>1</sub>. The critical curve centred on the first lens forms a pseudo-caustic the shape of which can be found by setting <em>r</em> \u2192 0 in equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>); this pseudo-caustic is shown in red in Figs\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a> and\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>. If \u03b2\u03b1<sub>1</sub>\u00a0\u226b\u00a0<em>y</em>/\u03b1<sub>2</sub>, the pseudo-caustic is approximately circular (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>.1); as <em>y</em> increases the pseudo-caustic becomes kidney bean shape (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>.3), until the pseudo-caustic loops back on itself to create a limacon (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>.4). The number of images does not increase if the source is within the loop of the pseudo-caustic compared to being entirely outside the limacon. The lower bound of equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ29\"></span><a href=\"#equ29\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">29</a>) corresponds to when the two circular caustics just touch (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>.2), and the upper bound corresponds to when the inner and outer loop of the limacon touch for a second time (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>.5). In Figs\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a> and\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>, we show the evolution of the limacon caustic as <em>y</em> and \u03b1<sub>2</sub> vary. If \u03b1<sub>2</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a0\u03b1<sub>1</sub>, being inside the limacon destroys an image rather than creating one (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>.6).</p>                        <a id=\"92096299\" scrollto-destination=\"92096299\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=jhC69ZLnwVNmEiiDE0AqjFMKE08cng3dfa3ykE3EqSphUruzzDDDT9LBDqYyuUI0pQPnOClqAabysYLngVR6LTUBVBTzXgzRZPyTDXP-fZs0R1Rgif8HfUd7L2yCwc2dIQSDcyRP8sam3~~9z9ghhKHijn~LdFf73MN~UmRiCg2PsSJ199KFftmF-W5drUtcTjJXPybq2Go8pJ~5vX0zR3E172REtnr0xX8skLrWZf~sl5PKsnlK5SevYV1RjbyY7CEho5mNlmfFNvu-SI1Ocvb0~LbUgEBERfYqvwpaO-iyEkgwakufvkHxFfaQCPFgVGVa~5eHWwPXtlnbQJ2opw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Topological comparison of the compound lens and the binary lens. The red pseudo-caustic corresponds to the critical curve at the core of the first lens (<em>r</em> \u2192 0), the circular black pseudo-caustics correspond to the critical curves at the cores of the images of the second lens. The red pseudo-caustic maps to a circle on the second lens plane, but is deformed by the lensing of the second lens. If the second lens is close to the red pseudo-caustic, it is deformed into a limacon on the source plane.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=FUKaaXfpNdf2rKSeLQTSae2LqlZ40~cWa-xkMjfGLLsTtmlIs4A2UYlY75e6tVGzDg~eMW0fCwDvjqEOddF4TGgQ22GY9KYnTFX1UbQ3kRYz9stFFhPvZcLvzoPKoCMmprJRS6cIC5wNhvgv-x3j1XBl7JrGi7oFgx6uXYDvhR8CK3-~xY2JjAJcQiqm~fF3LfHqm3Jn9c0qSFK5q9IRQLqdY7TJLJu2PGb7X1EsL3WXnkCFMGZOD7K3-kxsLZlzpV~W3GXMfbrEZc38djriLTL2WOt6nqlOgTJYEGl4ZNhd6VsvsW1-ruzLBFXIu~MOnwcRS0qSbhpOSMtx5AqXkw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The evolution of the pseudo-caustics of the compound lens as the offset between the two lenses increases, y\u00a0=\u00a00.01, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, 0.6. \u03b2\u00a0=\u00a00.5 and \u03b12\u00a0=\u00a00.6 are fixed. The red pseudo-caustic loops back on to itself when \u03b1<sub>2</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0\u03b1<sub>1</sub> at this point, being inside the red pseudo-caustic, decreases the number of images by one, as rays crossing the optical axis between the first and second lens can no longer be refocused by the first lens.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In addition to the Einstein zig-zag images, analogues of the off-axis images in Section\u00a03.1 can also form; again up to six images can be detectable. In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>, we show how the critical curves and caustics evolve as \u03b1<sub>2</sub> increases, holding the other parameters constant. For small \u03b1<sub>2</sub>, two off-axis caustics form; a six cusp \u2018star\u2019 caustic close to the optical axis and a four-cusp \u2018Concorde-shaped\u2019 caustic further out. The top panel of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a> shows a source covering the star caustic, which creates an Einstein ring around the primary lens but which bifurcates at the outer image of the second lens; this panel also shows a source covering the Concorde caustic, which creates a smaller Einstein ring around the inner image of the secondary lens. As \u03b1<sub>2</sub> increases to \u03b1<sub>2</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a0(1\u00a0\u2212\u00a0\u03b2)\u03b1<sub>1</sub>, these caustics go through metamorphoses; first a tricuspoid caustic pinches off from the \u2018star\u2019 caustic (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>, second panel), before merging into the \u2018Concorde\u2019 caustic to create a \u2018bat-shaped\u2019 caustic (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>, third and fourth panels). The evolution of these caustics are of more than academic interest since the cusps produce extreme magnifications, and caustics with cusps close to each other can produce much higher magnifications of extended sources than is possible with less exotic lenses. This is illustrated in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig7\" data-open=\"fig7\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">7</a> , where a source covers the tricuspoid caustic. A lens configuration this extreme requires some fine tuning of parameters, but a first lens with \u03c3<sub><em>V</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a0190\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.1 and a second lens with \u03c3<sub><em>V</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a0250\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.6 could magnify a <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a010 source with diameter 1\u00a0kpc by a factor of 110, or a source with diameter 0.25\u00a0kpc by a factor of 250.</p>                        <a id=\"92096303\" scrollto-destination=\"92096303\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig6\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 6.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig6.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=KFTUjf7tuABVSeghAnCBiKgYo6l3UjZtBmRmdagi7TQwCht-WCaz--pI-qBzelhvizvqIE~QksIqWOyebXe2y0M76JHB1s3130ewDg1DmT4hrOXpPsM3016UQc56FSfXjQiZKe2bekOowBmK~3BhtmKK6tzjPACSUWQTe2hxoE8RbmjsKo057bYbR6oyR3uUMZjCFY02bfpBNn4Wn4qX9IwOF2GmU5MmyCFva~J0F3r73p3S4SKmlw2panKNB2xyiMaH9Eeayqt~wzDMwE9K4ypxo96nqJTut0veeTNAQ2nLFy0CmXaBLquzMzEcVsWe153gWSOfwmHZmU1Pn4FhVw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The critical curve (left) and caustic structure (right) of a compound lens with \u03b11\u00a0=\u00a01, y\u00a0=\u00a00.2 and \u03b2\u00a0=\u00a00.9. It is clear from this, and Figs\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table the image configurations of many compound lenses are perturbed versions of classic lensing configurations, but for high-magnification and high-multiplicity events, the image configuration is very different to a typical galaxy\u2013galaxy or galaxy\u2013quasar strong lens.</p>                        <a id=\"92096306\" scrollto-destination=\"92096306\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig8\" class=\"fig for three different sets of \u03b1<sub>1</sub>, \u03b1<sub>2</sub>, <em>y</em>, \u03b2.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096307 id=\"92096307\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>BEYOND TWO SPHERICAL LENSES</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Massive galaxies in the real Universe can often deviate significantly from sphericity. Galaxy formation simulations show that the true mass distributions of galaxies are likely to be assymetric and clumpy (Vogelsberger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>); however, models of strong lensing events caused by elliptical galaxies show that elliptically symmetric mass profiles are able to reproduce the observed image positions in most galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses (e.g. Gavazzi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Kostrzewa-Rutkowska et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). If the first lens in a compound system is a singular isothermal ellipsoid, it is possible for up to four images of the second lens to form, as long as the second lens is close to the optical axis. In Section\u00a03.2, we found that for two zig-zag images to form it was necessary that the second lens be doubly imaged. If the second lens is quadruply imaged, one might naively expect, it to be possible to create four zig-zag images. In fact, lensing by two singular isothermal ellipsoid (SIE) profiles can generate as many as 16 images of a background source as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig9\" data-open=\"fig9\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">9</a>, where a 16<sup>+4</sup> image configuration is generated by an elliptical SIE with a second SIE almost directly behind it. The background source is also almost on the optical axis and the angle between the semimajor axes of the two lenses is only 10\u00a0deg. These high-multiplicity compound lenses often have exotic morphologies; we show a catalogue of some of them in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig12\" data-open=\"fig12\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">12</a>.</p>                        <a id=\"92096309\" scrollto-destination=\"92096309\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig9\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 9.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig9.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=a6hjySKY1ILTdEN1MvY0E4hkP92iZenX6XpLAhUg4k17UA8Bp8Bvm-PRji7RfMmfB0TL7IcfbjKlEFp9WomIawnQgY8xoa4KgYLgn9QzKCzjb7Rqvbooxcu1MHToTsE1QJJuSwyI1H3V7idIi7uG6dIG4emTfU4iEyBMufqTYmHtzFfat7NlGBSb3IMPrQ7DDw3~hOjIaZ3Cgi6vF4Q2wBCVmqBN621mLOcWJ6tWj8vh94WkGZveqiQEzT7~iPAh5dNuAx2gfVcMaquXaEmeE0qudgBN8b8nBJn4MXyUtu~7MQCalVy-I-qZHFSSo5wKy8Tdrww9dXTgSy8WrV6wTA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"A 16+4 image configuration caused by two isothermal ellipsoidal mass distributions. Such a high-multiplicity arrangement requires lenses and source to lie close to the optical axis, the major axis of the two lenses to be aligned and the second lens to satisfy equation (29). The 16 image configuration is therefore somewhat contrived. The black crosses show the location that images of a point source would form, while the central region of an extended source is shown in light blue, with the outer regions shown in red. If the second lens is associated with an extended light profile (assumed to be circular, with unlensed radius of 0.05 units), it would appear as the dark blue region. The centre and orientation of the primary lens are shown as the red cross, the unlensed centre and orientation of the second lens are shown as the dashed blue cross. The unlensed position of the background source is shown as a black circle. The parameters for this lens are $q_1=0.6,\\boldsymbol {y}=(0.01,0.01),q_2=0.6,\\theta _q=10^{\\circ },\\alpha _2=0.3,\\boldsymbol {z}=(-0.01,-0.01)$ (The first lens is centred at (0, 0) and aligned with the x-axis, \u03b11\u00a0=\u00a01).\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig9.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096309/stv2791fig9.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig9.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096309\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig9.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=sZLsh~uLsOIBSb49BRE3CYmjrkA622WiOATmqJpdl4Fn6bjcHCK-FCwc-ECka1tP9akOlaEkf0uoqERRx0cY21oW-RyFy-5mc0ltmszkoTR0TfwtfSLyYNgFkKmjb7nvnnJpuoH97DbyDEqASEnI170KPuzZziGQz6Xp40~YdERJpMtO2ZWfn7ADfBz2UBc98theCyDAowKjWSbuJtdSZKIribCJ3E6ujEDwK8fQy72xgJ7EEcHrMpJ0wHfL-QHIemTwCgEGeyN77vFKdQa4-UezbZ1~K-m26uXI2xFkxpW8aKDPMlZzYhMJoZBWP3~qNX6RgrRIeEA0yb1WymUhYg__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096309&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig9.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">A 16<sup>+4</sup> image configuration caused by two isothermal ellipsoidal mass distributions. Such a high-multiplicity arrangement requires lenses and source to lie close to the optical axis, the major axis of the two lenses to be aligned and the second lens to satisfy equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ29\"></span><a href=\"#equ29\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">29</a>). The 16 image configuration is therefore somewhat contrived.
