The black crosses show the location that images of a point source would form, while the central region of an extended source is shown in light blue, with the outer regions shown in red. If the second lens is associated with an extended light profile (assumed to be circular, with unlensed radius of 0.05 units), it would appear as the dark blue region. The centre and orientation of the primary lens are shown as the red cross, the unlensed centre and orientation of the second lens are shown as the dashed blue cross. The unlensed position of the background source is shown as a black circle. The parameters for this lens are <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$q_1=0.6,\\boldsymbol {y}=(0.01,0.01),q_2=0.6,\\theta _q=10^{\\circ },\\alpha _2=0.3,\\boldsymbol {z}=(-0.01,-0.01)$|</span> (The first lens is centred at (0, 0) and aligned with the <em>x</em>-axis, \u03b1<sub>1</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01).</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=92096310 id=\"92096310\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-1>Compound lensing events in the universe</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The previous sections have given us an intuitive feel for what compound lensing can produce; however, up to now, we have made no attempt to discuss how likely different image configurations are. To assess how common different compound lensing events are, we must first understand the populations of deflectors in the Universe. Collett (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) investigated the frequency of strong lensing events in forthcoming surveys. We use the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> code of Collett (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) to generate our population of first deflectors. The first deflector is drawn from the observed distribution of velocity dispersions and ellipticities observed in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (e.g. Choi, Park &amp; Vogeley <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>; Collett <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), with deflectors assumed to be randomly distributed in a comoving volume. The second deflector is then drawn from the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> source catalogue which is based upon semi-analytic models that paint sources on to the Millennium Simulation (see De Lucia et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Connolly et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>, for details), with number counts rescaled to match observations. The catalogue gives stellar masses that we convert into velocity dispersions using the relation of Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>); <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\log _{10}(\\sigma /\\mathrm{km\\,s^{-1}})=0.18 \\times \\log _{10}(M_{\\ast }/10^{11} \\,\\mathrm{M}_{{\\odot }})+2.34)$|\u2060</span>. We assume that the mass profile has the same alignment and flattening as the light profile. The <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> source catalogue includes both early and late-type galaxies from 0\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a06, whereas the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> deflector population only accounts for galaxies with \u03c3<sub><em>V</em></sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a0100 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> and <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a02. Our model's compound lens population therefore neglects any configurations where the first lens is low mass or at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a02.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The model of Collett (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) predicts 1.3\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>7</sup> strongly coupled compound deflectors per sky<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a>. For each compound deflector we define ten source planes at integer redshifts between 1 and 10 inclusive and place a single source within a projected radius of 1.2(\u03b8<sub><em>E</em>1</sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>)\u00a0+\u00a0\u03b8<sub><em>E</em>2</sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>)); we then numerically solve the compound lens equation to calculate how many images of the source form. To assess magnifications we assume an extended uniform disc source, and use ray shooting to estimate the area of the image plane that is filled by the source. We shoot rays at the image plane on a uniform square 1000\u00a0\u00d7\u00a01000 grid centred on the first lens and spaced by 0.006(\u03b8<sub><em>E</em>1</sub>\u00a0+\u00a0\u03b8<sub><em>E</em>2</sub>), and trace their path back on to the final source plane. We repeat this process for source planes at each integer redshift between 1 and 10. The optical depths are given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ36\" content-id=\"equ36\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\n\\tau (z_s) = N(z_s) \\times \\pi \\sum _i\\Big (1.2\\big (\\theta _{E1,i}(z_s)+\\theta _{E2,i}(z_s)\\big )\\Big )^2/A_{\\mathrm{sky}},\\nonumber\\\\\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(36)</span></div>where <em>N</em><sub><em>x</em></sub>(<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>) is the number of events observed in our model at that source redshift, and <em>A</em><sub>sky</sub> is the solid-angular extent of the sky.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">For computational speed and to ensure that the systems are at least plausibly resolved by current and next-generation telescopes, we neglect lenses that have \u03b2\u03b8<sub><em>E</em>1</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.2\u2009arcsec, \u03b8<sub><em>E</em>1</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.5\u2009arcsec or \u03b8<sub><em>E</em>2</sub>\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.1\u2009arcsec (where the Einstein radii are defined in terms of the source plane). We do not count images that have an (unsigned) magnification less than 0.1, as such demagnified images are likely to be hard to detect in practice. We count groups of multiple images separated by less than 0.1 arcsec as one image when assessing image multiplicity, as the scientific utility of such a group of images is likely to be the same as a single image, given the resolving power of current telescopes.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We find that the optical depth for compound lensing is roughly 6\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22126</sup> for <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a03. High-multiplicity images are rarer, but of order 4\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22128</sup> of the <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a03 sky is compound lensed to produce six or more images. The optical depth for multiple imaging is shown as a function of redshift in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig10\" data-open=\"fig10\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">10</a>. We find that the optical depth for compound lensing increases by an order of magnitude between <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a01 and 2 but is broadly flat between <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a04 and 10. Once magnification bias is included, we may expect to find such events in billion-galaxy surveys.</p>                        <a id=\"92096315\" scrollto-destination=\"92096315\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig10\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 10.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig10.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=rc0jjrpSIx0dMlpl4eCeyfFlmvLQqCPVLcazHCiNaCxkZ-ATQjYKLgvd9EkFWbNsfvB5HUE0WqhO~7TX7fpqkd0OiOA4UEZgXrSjngDMWvSOlGxDUvzrZOFzmWjUja6wcZhdCL07EMK-MDfYL1MTeXyWw1Vqlb~3Dr7daEr59gAaVQ-7gE2u4833Zhczzdk-e1p01umc-xbPJqcBNdYTR6i~qGKJnnNw8Um-wqkPnbsrIy9rCYBuYeXmcYZJrjODWSFJ9uZXBV8TPcO6oSBEFl3EyQgLjFuRxPQt8fGB6FfxR9kvrLS24q3zalEGZE36RbfmmNyH2QWznz25OKes7A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. Each coloured line represents a specific multiplicity of imaging as denoted by the number on the line.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig10.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096315/stv2791fig10.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig10.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096315\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig10.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=F8m5OL6iA6RGvjUVSVlfHEoVNrITC3MMjhk8~A1ZPFeIs4pz4VhX9NoF3iEgVAe1O46YJU~EhjZrgfJHPlisqrXjCAcGEeUjokzUQ161F5Dsk4EoiLlGkTGcI5MfpdQZjvqBOBkEToT2tKAzWFJwOJrQ5xUYrbsGrtlYSegZ6ocNtoI-rXZCbldMSovV-cP8S3yVcVKmtJmQbg7Y1mmiOCSNEoKzpaS9QV26mml4E3b0xOlnrJbDgRl~sHjtz3B5GVJWrxjme8lOSVtQLe5Szd~koIqokCovg5rBljT8CgakgjKvJqwqOx7DTY2tlfPqGGj2OmhuXfmPupLi27Hx~g__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096315&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig10.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. Each coloured line represents a specific multiplicity of imaging as denoted by the number on the line.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig10\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 10.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig10.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=rc0jjrpSIx0dMlpl4eCeyfFlmvLQqCPVLcazHCiNaCxkZ-ATQjYKLgvd9EkFWbNsfvB5HUE0WqhO~7TX7fpqkd0OiOA4UEZgXrSjngDMWvSOlGxDUvzrZOFzmWjUja6wcZhdCL07EMK-MDfYL1MTeXyWw1Vqlb~3Dr7daEr59gAaVQ-7gE2u4833Zhczzdk-e1p01umc-xbPJqcBNdYTR6i~qGKJnnNw8Um-wqkPnbsrIy9rCYBuYeXmcYZJrjODWSFJ9uZXBV8TPcO6oSBEFl3EyQgLjFuRxPQt8fGB6FfxR9kvrLS24q3zalEGZE36RbfmmNyH2QWznz25OKes7A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. The black solid line represents the total optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens. Each coloured line represents a specific multiplicity of imaging as denoted by the number on the line.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig10.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096315/stv2791fig10.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig10.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096315\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig10.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=F8m5OL6iA6RGvjUVSVlfHEoVNrITC3MMjhk8~A1ZPFeIs4pz4VhX9NoF3iEgVAe1O46YJU~EhjZrgfJHPlisqrXjCAcGEeUjokzUQ161F5Dsk4EoiLlGkTGcI5MfpdQZjvqBOBkEToT2tKAzWFJwOJrQ5xUYrbsGrtlYSegZ6ocNtoI-rXZCbldMSovV-cP8S3yVcVKmtJmQbg7Y1mmiOCSNEoKzpaS9QV26mml4E3b0xOlnrJbDgRl~sHjtz3B5GVJWrxjme8lOSVtQLe5Szd~koIqokCovg5rBljT8CgakgjKvJqwqOx7DTY2tlfPqGGj2OmhuXfmPupLi27Hx~g__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096315&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig10.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. The black solid line represents the total optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens. Each coloured line represents a specific multiplicity of imaging as denoted by the number on the line.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The optical depth for magnification by a compound lens is shown as a function of redshift in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig11\" data-open=\"fig11\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">11</a>. For extreme magnifications, we find that for sources with a 1\u00a0kpc radius compound lensing causes\u00a0\u223c10<sup>\u22126</sup> of sources at <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a02 to be increased by two or more magnitudes. Excitingly, a <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a010 source with radius 0.1\u00a0kpc will be magnified by 4 mag\u00a0\u223c10<sup>\u22127</sup> of the time, due to compound galaxy\u2013galaxy lensing. Due to the exponential cut-off of the high-redshift luminosity function, the population of high-<em>z</em> sources discovered in future surveys may be significantly biased towards extreme magnification events. Assuming that high-<em>z</em> sources are 0.1\u00a0kpc radius discs, and that the <em>Euclid</em> detection threshold for compound lenses is the same as for extended sources, the luminosity function of Mason, Trenti &amp; Treu (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) predicts <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$1.0_{-0.4}^{+0.5}$|</span> redshift 8 source that is magnified by 100 or more will be detectable with <em>Euclid</em> (Mason, private communication).</p>                        <a id=\"92096317\" scrollto-destination=\"92096317\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig11\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 11.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig11.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=Hm0izxsh9y5HdY2epSrPBukKQnkuqjQB9HSVO9Xe0CIUIE2Z8z4Hd~GPsXo-Xkk2lleMV2qbygd7fbRp2uDYzRGsBtChoBxjLY5NQTkkVIFY4CX8GhyPg9E3JAJNE9vxZG4dJD0znexdqUea8AViPpYPCGp-e3xXzXNKlhx83tetZw9nWM~ABpDCANTq6XmqlQivW8A64jte-lUbE9fMNAAkpyqAPxr1Zoe-BXBoL~oo~jcmhhTHiKI8HxKAcOBIDLp-zq~PYvB3KdOIvU9c5voiK8Ak8kP8nUwviefSsJmuePQDQKzHfh6wF~XDh7UYoaDDva2NF4S5kHpXyn5iTA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The optical depth for magnification causing changes in the apparent magnitude (\u0394m) of an extended source by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. The solid (dashed) lines are for a uniform circular source with radius 0.1\u00a0kpc (1\u00a0kpc).</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig11\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 11.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig11.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=Hm0izxsh9y5HdY2epSrPBukKQnkuqjQB9HSVO9Xe0CIUIE2Z8z4Hd~GPsXo-Xkk2lleMV2qbygd7fbRp2uDYzRGsBtChoBxjLY5NQTkkVIFY4CX8GhyPg9E3JAJNE9vxZG4dJD0znexdqUea8AViPpYPCGp-e3xXzXNKlhx83tetZw9nWM~ABpDCANTq6XmqlQivW8A64jte-lUbE9fMNAAkpyqAPxr1Zoe-BXBoL~oo~jcmhhTHiKI8HxKAcOBIDLp-zq~PYvB3KdOIvU9c5voiK8Ak8kP8nUwviefSsJmuePQDQKzHfh6wF~XDh7UYoaDDva2NF4S5kHpXyn5iTA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The optical depth for magnification causing changes in the apparent magnitude (\u0394m) of an extended source by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. The solid (dashed) lines are for a uniform circular source with radius 0.1\u00a0kpc (1\u00a0kpc).\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig11.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096317/stv2791fig11.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig11.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096317\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig11.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=ldbR8APNb5kS6rIs0NxjO6OZkhYM5BXJm2HAJMZd11zcL1WBspVc65zv2Fj4t8Wxib3-N1cRMleKQZKq10xXQcRKqkODXBFvIhM9NwlFgtMg5tD4wLsu7XPbbzmbpHm-2e09URu3SkewaBRphss6xrOmqE1k0arWXwr4mEbSL1tfk5xTzVM4gdKiFOGQerQPSvqE2h1fLyJtnum0lQq7mrf4e2gxJ3TK3HE21KSuQ6q~zFc1gTocogxl0D2WNNA4v8tlu2~lG6SqNoT9iiKO63NrkSrnzXo~a~s4koBsw8ub1pbX5S-O8ozgSs739HMGsEE4eP6OceRr2X0aHZgSUg__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096317&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig11.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The optical depth for magnification causing changes in the apparent magnitude (\u0394m) of an extended source by a compound lens as a function of source redshift. The solid (dashed) lines are for a uniform circular source with radius 0.1\u00a0kpc (1\u00a0kpc).</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096318 id=\"92096318\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>CONCLUSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we have investigated compound lensing by two lens galaxies and a further source, at different redshifts, and the image morphologies this can generate. We found that compound lensing can produce high-multiplicity and high-magnification events. Discovering a high-multiplicity lensed quasar would be an extremely powerful cosmological probe, since many independent time-delays can be measured. High-magnification events have the power to uncover the population of ultrafaint high-redshift sources that are otherwise far beyond the depth and resolution of current telescopes.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We found analytic results for the case of two SIS lenses, solving completely for the case when the observer, source and both lenses lie on a plane. From this, we found that the highest multiplicity images can only form if both (a) the second lens is multiply imaged by the first lens and (b) the Einstein radius of the second lens is comparable to, but less than the Einstein radius of the first lens. We showed numerically that when the source lies off of the plane, some lens configurations can generate higher order catastrophes that produce extreme magnifications \u2013 a potentially important method for discovering and studying intrinsically faint high-redshift sources. The simple picture painted by the two SIS model is complicated significantly when the lenses are elliptically flattened. Up to 16 image configurations can exist if the second lens is quadruply imaged by the first lens.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">With this analytic understanding of the system, we then used the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> code of Collett (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) to answers our three primary questions. </p><ul class=\"bullet\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">How often does compound lensing generate high-multiplicity systems? We found that the optical depth for multiple imaging by a compound lens is \u2243 6\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22126</sup> for 3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a010. Image multiplicities of six or greater are\u00a0\u223c2.2 orders of magnitude rarer. A wide-deep survey like LSST which should detect\u00a0\u223c10<sup>10</sup> galaxies beyond redshift 2 (Ivezic et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>), and is therefore likely to include some high-multiplicity lenses. This may be an underestimate, since arguments based on optical depths do not include magnification bias.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">How often does compound lensing generate extreme magnifications? We found \u22432\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>\u22126</sup> of the 3\u00a0&lt;\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a010 sky has its flux increased by more than 2 mag. Magnification of 100 (decreasing the apparent magnitude by five) is 2.5 orders of magnitude rarer for 0.1\u00a0kpc radius sources, but is almost impossible for sources with 1\u00a0kpc radii. Under simplifying assumptions, we inferred that <em>Euclid</em> may discover one <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a08 source that has been magnified by a hundred.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">What do high-multiplicity/magnification compound lenses look like?</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For low-multiplicity/magnification compound lenses, the image configurations are typically perturbed versions of single-plane image morphologies. However, for high-multiplicity/magnification compound lenses, the image morphologies are significantly more complicated; even with two SIE profiles the parameter space is rich, and many images can form. We show 20 high-multiplicity configurations in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig12\" data-open=\"fig12\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">12</a>.</p></li></ul>                        <a id=\"92096323\" scrollto-destination=\"92096323\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig12\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 12.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig12.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=2RxCsGAqVN-Ler~zt426Sn-xHdZiVHNEHiWDdI9P5Zx0-Vwp7~TU~qdpPTYPjcYGY0JO92mB-r2ay5-Gk1S-B27f8baW~rC64nowYh63Y2-pDpf~EmoLsCFgbT88OSL7t4Cv2LRGNKbtpWRpFPWF~4tBcyXolFJmYnJe102H1tBheX5oZuRA4yBwhrS-Zjuo6TtihcaF5Ey1Ll9C0FbpTUrHPTat2ASlsZBRtxws1lmma2KOhU68tc356-WKCQt6mBUgAXl69jGOCtz6v7MpQ7eQA~20RXwOEDvsZU-ML~pEWWgTNKrZoUleVF5e-NoiQAwdNRVTDj6W6vG6jOKuyw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"A montage of high-multiplicity compound lenses generated from the lenspop population of plausible deflectors. The black crosses show the location that images of a point source would form, while the central region of an extended source is shown in light blue, with the outer regions shown in red. If the second lens is associated with an extended light profile (assumed to be circular), it would appear as the dark blue region. The optical centre of the first lens is shown as a red cross.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096323/stv2791fig12.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096323\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig12.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=lp38igRDBDmZGr8UbCNwElXfSxEySbDtZWTrKpsLbots0pU4Jho8TIC9RZDhW~YIq-K7O0s1LYuzQZ5xCtWyz~V-sQtj~aELFaHNnghbX5FtHw~GYy-hTCY6BI5FnfhyDfG8Ty~CAtwLGRtAoUyDPshDVQ22AOCcIbObuok4VEwerYfy7OeP2pPgDTeXmG~9CqT0o2dqSvr1HqUEZ5OCSe-MHE5NhEn-hp2mk5DvK8YtFNW8zmT~seoBxVCOGw7JhQfphaMcOJtz6bDWqBqW31H1zh3Mq8q~6-G9y9UIqP3Lf31HI1Il2gWnwzcqoMzcvirYwg5Aha3tpCG6Ji7eiA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096323&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">A montage of high-multiplicity compound lenses generated from the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> population of plausible deflectors. The black crosses show the location that images of a point source would form, while the central region of an extended source is shown in light blue, with the outer regions shown in red. If the second lens is associated with an extended light profile (assumed to be circular), it would appear as the dark blue region. The optical centre of the first lens is shown as a red cross.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig12\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 12.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/m_stv2791fig12.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&amp;Signature=2RxCsGAqVN-Ler~zt426Sn-xHdZiVHNEHiWDdI9P5Zx0-Vwp7~TU~qdpPTYPjcYGY0JO92mB-r2ay5-Gk1S-B27f8baW~rC64nowYh63Y2-pDpf~EmoLsCFgbT88OSL7t4Cv2LRGNKbtpWRpFPWF~4tBcyXolFJmYnJe102H1tBheX5oZuRA4yBwhrS-Zjuo6TtihcaF5Ey1Ll9C0FbpTUrHPTat2ASlsZBRtxws1lmma2KOhU68tc356-WKCQt6mBUgAXl69jGOCtz6v7MpQ7eQA~20RXwOEDvsZU-ML~pEWWgTNKrZoUleVF5e-NoiQAwdNRVTDj6W6vG6jOKuyw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"A montage of high-multiplicity compound lenses generated from the lenspop population of plausible deflectors. The black crosses show the location that images of a point source would form, while the central region of an extended source is shown in light blue, with the outer regions shown in red. If the second lens is associated with an extended light profile (assumed to be circular), it would appear as the dark blue region. The optical centre of the first lens is shown as a red cross.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/92096323/stv2791fig12.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"92096323\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/456/2/10.1093_mnras_stv2791/1/stv2791fig12.jpeg?Expires=1611467194&Signature=lp38igRDBDmZGr8UbCNwElXfSxEySbDtZWTrKpsLbots0pU4Jho8TIC9RZDhW~YIq-K7O0s1LYuzQZ5xCtWyz~V-sQtj~aELFaHNnghbX5FtHw~GYy-hTCY6BI5FnfhyDfG8Ty~CAtwLGRtAoUyDPshDVQ22AOCcIbObuok4VEwerYfy7OeP2pPgDTeXmG~9CqT0o2dqSvr1HqUEZ5OCSe-MHE5NhEn-hp2mk5DvK8YtFNW8zmT~seoBxVCOGw7JhQfphaMcOJtz6bDWqBqW31H1zh3Mq8q~6-G9y9UIqP3Lf31HI1Il2gWnwzcqoMzcvirYwg5Aha3tpCG6Ji7eiA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=92096323&ar=1069840&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv2791fig12.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">A montage of high-multiplicity compound lenses generated from the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> population of plausible deflectors. The black crosses show the location that images of a point source would form, while the central region of an extended source is shown in light blue, with the outer regions shown in red. If the second lens is associated with an extended light profile (assumed to be circular), it would appear as the dark blue region. The optical centre of the first lens is shown as a red cross.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this work, we have assumed a population of isothermal ellipsoids in an otherwise homogeneous Universe. Deviations from isothermality and elliptical symmetry and the perturbative effects of mass along the line of sight will further increase the complexity of compound lenses that are possible in the real Universe. Such complexity is beyond the scope of this work, but we note that in principle this can further increase magnifications and image multiplicity. Where lenses are less steeply cusped the pseudo-caustics in Figs\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a> and\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a> become classical caustics, and it is possible for detectable central images to form near the core of the primary lens or the images of the secondary. For aligned spherical lenses, an Einstein ring forms at \u03b8<sub><em>E</em>1</sub>\u00a0+\u00a0\u03b8<sub><em>E</em>2</sub> if both lenses are isothermal; the radius is larger if both lenses are subisothermal. At small impact parameters, the deflection angles are increased for steeper-than-isothermal lenses. We therefore expect that shallower profiles are more efficient at producing low-multiplicity compound lenses, while steeper profiles produce more Einstein zig-zags.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">There is a real danger that current lens finding algorithms would fail to detect any of the high-multiplicity compound lenses shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig12\" data-open=\"fig12\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">12</a>. Strong lens finders based on forward modelling such as that in Brault &amp; Gavazzi (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) would not be able to fit most of the compound lenses with a single-plane lens model. Compound lenses may be more easily found by finders that look for ring- or arc-like features such as Gavazzi et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) or Seidel &amp; Bartelmann (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>), since higher multiplicity lenses imply more chances for the features to be present. The main problem however is sample purity; the rarity of strong lensing means that most lens finders will severely cut the pool of candidates to remove the large numbers of false positives. Algorithms that remove false positives from lens samples are likely to also remove some of the compound lenses. The most exotic lenses will require novel discovery algorithms, or an extensive and imaginative human search.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We are grateful to Charlotte Mason for calculating the size of the compound lens population discoverable by <em>Euclid</em>. We thank the anonymous referee for an extremely constructive report on the original manuscript. We are grateful to Charlotte Mason and Bob Nichol for useful discussions and comments on the draft manuscript. We are grateful to Matt Auger for the lensing deflections code. We are grateful to Thomas Tram for improvements to our caustic tracing code. This work has used the LSST sky simulations; we are grateful to Andy Connolly for sharing these, and to Scott Daniel and Gary Burton for enabling access to them. This project made use of the <span class=\"small-caps\">lenspop</span> code which is freely available from github.com/tcollett/LensPop. Numerical computations were done on the Sciama High Performance Compute (HPC) cluster which is supported by the ICG, SEPNet and the University of Portsmouth.</p><div content-id=\"fn1\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn1\" class=\"end-note-link\">1</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">Rearranging equation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ21\"></span><a href=\"#equ21\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">21</a>) into a single equation gives an eighth-order polynomial.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn2\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn2\" class=\"end-note-link\">2</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">A pseudo-caustic is a caustic, but crossing it only creates one detectable additional image, since the second additional image is infinitely demagnified at the core of a singularity.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn3\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn3\" class=\"end-note-link\">3</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">That is to say, there are 1.3\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>7</sup> strong lenses in the Universe with second components sufficiently bright and massive to be included in the catalogue of Connolly et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) \u2013 of course most are undetectable with current instrumentation.</p></div></span></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=92096332 id=\"92096332\" class=\"backreferences-title\" >REFERENCES</h2>\r\n<div class=\"ref-list\"><div content-id=\"bib1\" data-legacy-id=\"bib1\"><div class=\"refLink-parent\"><span class=\"refLink\"><a name=\"jumplink-bib1\" href=\"javascript:;\" aria-label=\"jumplink-bib1\"></a></span></div><div class=\"ref false\"><div id=\"ref-auto-bib1\" class=\"ref-content \"><div class=\"mixed-citation citation\"><p class=\"mixed-citation-compatibility\">\r\n<span class=\"person-group\">\r\n<div class=\"name\">\r\n<div class=\"surname\">Auger</div>\r\n <div class=\"given-names\">M. "Scanned", "Action": "Scanned link"}}, "Tables status": "Empty", "Paper Overview": {"type_of_reference": "JOUR", "Title": "Compound lensing: Einstein zig-zags and high-multiplicity lensed images", "authors": ["Collett, Thomas E.", "Bacon, David J."], "author_address": ["AA(Institute of Cosmology and Gravitation, University of Portsmouth, Burnaby Rd, Portsmouth PO1 3FX, UK), AB(Institute of Cosmology and Gravitation, University of Portsmouth, Burnaby Rd, Portsmouth PO1 3FX, UK)"], "journal_name": "Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society", "volume": "456", "publication_year": "2016/02/1", "start_page": "2210", "end_page": "2220", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing: strong", "Astrophysics - Cosmology and Nongalactic Astrophysics"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2016MNRAS.456.2210C", "abstract": "Compound strong gravitational lensing is a rare phenomenon, but a handful of such lensed systems are likely to be discovered in forthcoming surveys. In this work, we use a double singular isothermal sphere lens model to analytically understand how the properties of the system impact image multiplicity for the final source. We find that up to six images of a background source can form, but only if the second lens is multiply imaged by the first and the Einstein radius of the second lens is comparable to, but does not exceed that of the first. We then build a model of compound lensing masses in the Universe, using singular isothermal ellipsoid (SIE) lenses, and assess how the optical depth for multiple imaging by a galaxy-galaxy compound lens varies with source redshift. For a source redshift of 4, we find optical depths of 6 \u00d7 10<SUP>-6</SUP> for multiple imaging and 5 \u00d7 10<SUP>-8</SUP> for multiplicity of 6 or greater. We find that extreme magnifications are possible, with magnifications of 100 or more for 6 \u00d7 10<SUP>-9</SUP> of z = 10 sources with 0.1 kpc radii. We show some of the image configurations that can be generated by compound lenses, and demonstrate that they are qualitatively different to those generated by single-plane lenses; dedicated compound lens finders will be necessary if these systems are to be discovered in forthcoming surveys.", "doi": "10.1093/mnras/stv2791", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1510.00242", "issn": "0035-8711", "number": "2"}}
